Copan, Undersecretary of Commerce for Standards and Technology and DirectorNIST SPECIAL PUBLICATION 1800-8A Securing Wireless Infusion Pumps in Healthcare Delivery Organizations Volume A: Executive Summary Gavin O’Brien National Cybersecurity Center of Excellence Information Technology Laboratory Sallie Edwards Kevin Littlefield Neil McNab Sue Wang Kangmin Zheng The MITRE Corporation McLean, VA August 2018 This publication is available free of charge from: https://doi.org/10.6028/NIST.SP.1800-8 The first draft of this publication is available free of charge from: https://www.nccoe.nist.gov/sites/default/files/library/sp1800/hit-infusion-pump-nist-sp1800-8- draft.pdfExecutive Summary ▪ Broad technological advancements have contributed to the Internet of Things (IoT) phenomenon, where physical devices now have technology that allow them to connect to the internet and communicate with other devices or systems. With billions of devices being connected to the internet, many industries, including healthcare, have leveraged, or are beginning to leverage, IoT devices to improve operational efficiency and enhance innovation. ▪ Medical devices, such as infusion pumps, were once standalone instruments that interacted only with the patient or medical provider. With technological improvements designed to enhance patient care, these devices now connect wirelessly to a variety of systems, networks, and other tools within a healthcare delivery organization (HDO)—ultimately contributing to the Internet of Medical Things (IoMT). ▪ As IoMT grows, cybersecurity risks have risen. According to the Association for the Advancement of Medical Instrumentation (AAMI) Technical Information Report 57 (TIR57), “this has created a new source of risk for [the] safe operation [of medical devices].” In particular, the wireless infusion pump ecosystem (the pump, the network, and the data stored in and on a pump) faces a range of threats, including unauthorized access to protected health information (PHI), changes to prescribed drug doses, and interference with a pump’s function. ▪ In addition to managing interconnected medical devices, HDOs oversee complex, highly technical environments, from back-office applications for billing and insurance services, supply chain and inventory management, and staff scheduling, to clinical systems, such as radiological and pharmaceutical support. In this intricate healthcare environment, HDOs and medical device manufacturers that share responsibility and take a collaborative, holistic approach to reducing cybersecurity risks of the infusion pump ecosystem can better protect healthcare systems, patients, PHI, and enterprise information. ▪ The National Cybersecurity Center of Excellence (NCCoE) at the National Institute of Standards and Technology (NIST) analyzed risk factors in and around the infusion pump ecosystem by using a questionnaire-based risk assessment. With the results of that assessment, the NCCoE then developed an example implementation that demonstrates how HDOs can use standards-based, commercially available cybersecurity technologies to better protect the infusion pump ecosystem, including patient information and drug library dosing limits. CHALLENGE Technology improvements happen rapidly across all sectors. For organizations focused on streamlining operations and delivering high-quality patient care, it can be difficult to take advantage of the latest technological advances, while also ensuring that new medical devices or applications are secure. For many HDOs, this can result in improperly configured information technology networks and components that increase cybersecurity risks. Unlike prior medical devices that were once standalone instruments, today’s wireless infusion pumps connect to a variety of healthcare systems, networks, and other devices. Although connecting infusion pumps to point-of-care medication systems and electronic health records (EHRs) can improve healthcare delivery processes, using a medical device’s connectivity capabilities can create significant cybersecurity risk, which could lead to operational or safety risks. Tampering, intentional or otherwise, with the NIST SP 1800-8A: Securing Wireless Infusion Pumps 1 This publication is available free of charge from: https://doi.org/10.6028/NIST.SP.1800-8.wireless infusion pump ecosystem can expose a healthcare provider’s enterprise to serious risks, such as the following examples: ▪ access by malicious actors ▪ loss or corruption of enterprise information and patient data and health records ▪ a breach of PHI ▪ loss or disruption of healthcare services ▪ damage to an organization’s reputation, productivity, and bottom-line revenue As IoMT grows, with an increasing number of infusion pumps connecting to networks, the vulnerabilities and risk factors become more critical, as they can expose the pump ecosystem to external attacks, compromises, or interference. SOLUTION The NCCoE has developed cybersecurity guidance, NIST Special Publication (SP) 1800-8: Securing Wireless Infusion Pumps, by using standards-based commercially available technologies and industry best practices to help HDOs strengthen the security of the wireless infusion pump ecosystem within healthcare facilities. This NIST cybersecurity publication provides best practices and detailed guidance on how to manage assets, protect against threats, and mitigate vulnerabilities by performing a questionnaire-based risk assessment. In addition, the security characteristics of the wireless infusion pump ecosystem are mapped to currently available cybersecurity standards and the Health Insurance Portability and Accountability Act (HIPAA) Security Rule. Based on our risk assessment findings, we apply security controls to the pump’s ecosystem to create a “defense-in-depth” solution for protecting infusion pumps and their surrounding systems against various risk factors. Ultimately, we show how biomedical, networking, and cybersecurity engineers and IT professionals can securely configure and deploy wireless infusion pumps to reduce cybersecurity risk. While the NCCoE used a suite of commercial products to address this challenge, this guide does not endorse these particular products, nor does it guarantee compliance with any regulatory initiatives. Your organization's information security experts should identify the products that will best integrate with your existing tools and IT system infrastructure. Your organization can adopt this solution or one that adheres to these guidelines in whole, or you can use this guide as a starting point for tailoring and implementing parts of a solution. BENEFITS The NCCoE’s practice guide to securing wireless infusion pumps in HDOs can help your organization: ▪ reduce cybersecurity risk, and potentially reduce impact to safety and operational risk, such as the loss of patient information or interference with the standard operation of a medical device ▪ develop and execute a defense-in-depth strategy that protects the enterprise with layers of security to avoid a single point of failure and provide strong support for availability NIST SP 1800-8A: Securing Wireless Infusion Pumps 2 This publication is available free of charge from: https://doi.org/10.6028/NIST.SP.1800-8.▪ implement current cybersecurity standards and best practices, while maintaining the performance and usability of wireless infusion pumps SHARE YOUR FEEDBACK You can view or download the guide at https://www.nccoe.nist.gov/projects/use-cases/medical-devices. We recognize that technical solutions alone will not fully enable the benefits of our solution, so we encourage organizations to share lessons learned and best practices for transforming the processes associated with implementing this guide. SI T S. 1.P ABSTRACT 8 0 0 -8 Medical devices, such as infusion pumps, were once standalone instruments that interacted only with . the patient or medical provider. However, today’s medical devices connect to a variety of healthcare systems, networks, and other tools within a healthcare delivery organization (HDO). Connecting devices to point-of-care medication systems and electronic health records can improve healthcare delivery processes; however, increasing connectivity capabilities also creates cybersecurity risks. Potential threats include unauthorized access to patient health information, changes to prescribed drug doses, and interference with a pump’s function. The NCCoE at NIST analyzed risk factors in and around the infusion pump ecosystem by using a questionnaire-based risk assessment to develop an example implementation that demonstrates how HDOs can use standards-based, commercially available cybersecurity technologies to better protect the infusion pump ecosystem, including patient information and drug library dosing limits. With billions of devices being connected to the internet, many industries, including healthcare, have or are beginning to leverage IoT devices to improve operational efficiency and enhance innovation. Medical devices, such as infusion pumps, were once standalone instruments that interacted only with T h the patient or medical provider . With technological improvements designed to enhance patient care, si p these devices now connect wirelessly to a variety of systems, networks, and other tools within a u b cil healthcare delivery organization (HDO)—ultimately contributing to the Internet of Medical Things a t oi (IoMT). The wireless infusion pump ecosystem (the pump, the network, and the data stored in and on a n si pump) faces a range of threats, including unauthorized access to protected health information (PHI), a v a changes to prescribed drug doses, and interference with a pump’s function. ali b el rf In addition to managing interconnected medical devices, HDOs oversee complex, highly technical e e environments, from back-office applications for billing and insurance services, supply chain and o c f inventory management, and staff scheduling, to clinical systems, such as radiological and h a gr pharmaceutical support. In this intricate healthcare environment, HDOs and medical device e rf manufacturers that share responsibility and take a collaborative, holistic approach to reducing o m h : cybersecurity risks of the wireless infusion pump ecosystem can better protect healthcare systems, t pt patients, protected health information (PHI), and enterprise information. s /: / d o The National Cybersecurity Center of Excellence (NCCoE) at the National Institute of Standards and o.i r Technology (NIST) developed an example implementation that demonstrates how HDOs can use g / 01 standards-based, commercially available cybersecurity technologies to better protect the wireless 6. 0 infusion pump ecosystem, including patient information and drug library dosing limits. 2 8 / N SI The NCCoE’s project has resulted in a NIST Cybersecurity Practice Guide, Securing Wireless Infusion T PS. Pumps in Healthcare Delivery Organizations, that addresses how to manage this challenge in clinical 1. 8 settings, with a reference design and example implementation. Our example solution starts with two 0 0 -8 types of risk assessments: an industry analysis of risk and a questionnaire-based-risk assessment. With . the results of that assessment, we then used a “defense-in-depth” strategy to secure the pump, server components, and surrounding network to create a better-protected environment for wireless infusion pumps. The solution and architecture presented in this guide are built upon standards-based, commercially available products and represent one of many possible solutions and architectures. The example implementation can be used by any organization that is deploying wireless infusion pump systems and that is willing to perform its own risk assessment and implement controls based on its risk posture. NIST SP 1800-8B: Securing Wireless Infusion Pumps 21.1 Challenge The Food and Drug Administration (FDA) defines an external infusion pump as a medical device that delivers fluids into a patient’s body in a controlled manner, using interconnected servers or via a standalone drug library-based medication delivery system . In the past, infusion pumps were standalone instruments that interacted only with the patient and the medical provider. Now, connecting infusion pumps to point-of-care medication systems and electronic health records (EHRs) can help improve healthcare delivery processes, but using a medical device’s connectivity capabilities can also create cybersecurity risk, which could lead to operational or safety risks. T h si Wireless infusion pumps are challenging to protect, for several reasons. They can be infected by p u b malware, which can cause them to malfunction or operate differently than originally intended, and cil a traditional malware protection could negatively impact the pump’s ability to operate efficiently. In t oi n addition, most wireless infusion pumps contain a maintenance default passcode. If HDOs do not change si a the default passcodes when provisioning pumps, and do not periodically change the passwords after v a ali pumps are deployed, this creates a vulnerability. This can make it difficult to revoke access codes b rf el (e.g., when a hospital employee resigns from the job). Furthermore, information stored inside infusion e e pumps must be properly secured, including data from drug library systems, infusion rates and dosages, o c f or PHI , , , , . h a r g e Additionally, like other devices with operating systems and software that connect to a network, the rf mo wireless infusion pump ecosystem creates a large attack surface (i.e., the different points where an h : attacker could get into a system, and where they could exfiltrate data out), primarily due to t t p s vulnerabilities in operating systems, subsystems, networks, or default configuration settings that allow /: / d for possible unauthorized access , , . Because many infusion pump models can be accessed and o ro.i programmed remotely through a healthcare facility’s wireless network, this vulnerability could be g / 1 exploited to allow an unauthorized user to interfere with the pump’s function, harming a patient 0 6. 0 through incorrect drug dosing or the compromise of that patient’s PHI. 2 8 / N SI These risk factors are real, exposing the wireless pump ecosystem to external attacks, compromise, or T S. interference , , . Digital tampering, intentional or otherwise, with a wireless infusion pump’s P 1. 8 ecosystem (the pump, the network, and data in and on the pump) can expose an HDO to critical risk 0 0 -8 factors, such as malicious actors; loss of data; a breach of PHI; loss of services; loss of health records; the . potential for downtime; and damage to an HDO’s reputation, productivity, and bottom-line revenue. This practice guide helps you address your assets, threats, and vulnerabilities by demonstrating how to perform a questionnaire-based risk assessment survey. After you complete the assessment, you can apply security controls to the infusion pumps in your area of responsibility to create a defense-in-depth solution to protect them from cybersecurity risks. NIST SP 1800-8B: Securing Wireless Infusion Pumps 31.2 Solution The NIST Cybersecurity Practice Guide Securing Wireless Infusion Pumps in Healthcare Delivery Organizations shows how biomedical engineers, networking engineers, security engineers, and IT professionals, using commercially available and open-source tools and technologies that are consistent with cybersecurity standards, can help securely configure and deploy wireless infusion pumps within HDOs. In addition, the security characteristics of the wireless infusion pump ecosystem are mapped to currently available cybersecurity standards and the Health Insurance Portability and Accountability Act T h si (HIPAA) Security Rule. In developing our solution, we used standards and guidance from the following p u b sources: cil a t oi ▪ NIST Framework for Improving Critical Infrastructure Cybersecurity  n si a ▪ NIST Risk Management Framework (RMF) , ,  v a bali ▪ NIST SP 800-53 Revision 4: Security and Privacy Controls for Federal Information Systems and el rf Organizations  e e o ▪ Association for the Advancement of Medical Instrumentation (AAMI) Technical Information c f h Report (TIR)57  a r g e rf ▪ International Electrotechnical Commission Technical Report (IEC/TR) 80001-2: Application of risk o m management for IT-networks incorporating medical devices , , , ,  h : ptt ▪ FDA’s Postmarket Management of Cybersecurity in Medical Devices  s /: / d Ultimately, this practice guide: o o.i gr ▪ maps security characteristics to standards and best practices from NIST and other standards / 1 0 organizations, as well as to the HIPAA Security Rule , , , ,  6. 0 2 8 ▪ provides a detailed architecture and capabilities that address security controls / N TSI ▪ provides a how-to for implementers and security engineers to recreate the reference design S. To accomplish this, medical devices now contain operating P 1. systems and communication hardware that allow them to connect to networks and other devices. The 8 0 0 connected functionality responsible for much of the improvement of medical devices poses challenges -8 . not formerly seen with standalone instruments. Clinicians and patients rely on infusion pumps for a safe and accurate administration of fluids and medications. However, the FDA has identified problems that can compromise the safe use of external infusion pumps , , . These issues can lead to over-infusion or under-infusion, missed treatments, or delayed therapy. The NCCoE initiated this project to help healthcare providers develop a more secure wireless infusion pump ecosystem, which can be applied to similarly connected medical devices. The wireless infusion pump was selected as a representative medical device. Throughout the remainder of this guide, the focus will be on the secure operation of the wireless infusion pump ecosystem. Both the NIST SP 1800-8B: Securing Wireless Infusion Pumps 7architecture and security controls may be applied to increase the security posture for other types of medical devices. However, any application should be reviewed and tailored to the specific environment in which the medical device will operate. Throughout the wireless infusion pump project, we collaborated with our healthcare Community of Interest (COI) and cybersecurity vendors to identify infusion pump threat actors, define interactions between the actors and systems, review risk factors, develop an architecture and reference design, identify applicable mitigating security technologies, and design an example implementation. This practice guide highlights the approach used to develop the NCCoE reference solution. Elements include T risk assessment and analysis, logical design, build development, test and evaluation, and security control h si p mapping. This practice guide seeks to help the healthcare community evaluate the security environment u b cil surrounding infusion pumps deployed in a clinical setting. a t oi n si 3.1 Audience a v a ali This guide is primarily intended for professionals implementing security solutions within an HDO. It may b el also be of interest to anyone responsible for securing non-traditional computing devices (i.e., the rf ee Internet of Things [IoT]). o c f h More specifically, Volume B of the practice guide (NIST SP 1800-8B) is designed to appeal to a wide a r eg range of job functions. This volume provides cybersecurity or technology decision makers within HDOs rf o with a view into how they can make the medical device environment more secure to help improve their m h : enterprise’s security posture and help reduce enterprise risk. Additionally, this volume offers technical t t sp staff guidance on architecting a more secure medical device network and instituting compensating /: / d controls. o o.i r g / 3.2 Scope 1 0 6. 0 2 The NCCoE project focused on securing the environment of the medical device and not re-engineering 8 / N the device itself. To do this, we reviewed known vulnerabilities in wireless infusion pumps and examined SI T S. how the architecture and component integration could be designed to increase the security of the P 1. device. The approach considered the life cycle of a wireless infusion pump, from planning the purchase 8 0 0 to decommissioning, with a concentration on the configuration, use, and maintenance phases. -8 . 3.3 Assumptions Considerable research, investigation, and collaboration went into the development of the reference design in this guide. The actual build and example implementation of this architecture occurred in a lab environment at the NCCoE. Although the lab is based on a clinical environment, it does not mirror the complexity of an actual hospital network. It is assumed that any actual clinical environment would represent additional complexity. NIST Special Publication (SP) 800-30, Guide for Conducting Risk Assessments, states that risk is “a 8 0 0 measure of the extent to which an entity is threatened by a potential circumstance or event, and -8 . typically a function of (i) the adverse impacts that would arise if the circumstance or event occurs and (ii) the likelihood of occurrence” . The guide further defines risk assessment as “the process of identifying, estimating, and prioritizing risks to organizational operations (including mission, functions, image, reputation), organizational assets, individuals, other organizations, and the Nation, resulting from the operation of an information system. Part of risk management incorporates threat and vulnerability analyses, and considers mitigations provided by security controls planned or in place.” The NCCoE recommends that any discussion of risk management, particularly at the enterprise level, begins with a comprehensive review of NIST SP 800-37, Guide for Applying the Risk Management NIST SP 1800-8B: Securing Wireless Infusion Pumps 9Framework to Federal Information Systems—material that is available to the public . The risk management framework (RMF) guidance, as a whole, proved to be invaluable in giving us a baseline to assess risks, from which we developed the project, the security characteristics of the build, and this guide. It is important to understand what constitutes the definition of risk, as it relates to non-traditional information systems, such as wireless infusion pumps. P 1. 8 0 0 -8 . 4.1 Risk Assessments For this NCCoE project, we performed two types of risk assessments: an industry analysis of risk and a questionnaire-based risk assessment. NIST SP 1800-8B: Securing Wireless Infusion Pumps 114.1.1 Industry Analysis of Risk The first assessment was an industry analysis of risk performed while developing the initial use case. This industry analysis provided insight into the challenges of integrating medical devices into a clinical environment containing a standard IT network. Completion of the industry analysis narrowed the objective of our use case to helping HDOs secure medical devices on an enterprise network, with a specific focus on wireless infusion pumps. Activities involved in our industry analysis included reaching out to our COI and other industry experts through workshops and focus group discussions. After receiving feedback on the NCCoE’s use case T h si publication through a period of public comment, the NCCoE adjudicated the comments and clarified a p bu project description. These activities were instrumental to identifying primary risk factors as well as cil a educating our team on the uniqueness of cybersecurity risks involved in protecting medical devices in t oi n healthcare environments. si a v a ali 4.1.2 Questionnaire-Based Risk Assessment b el rf For the second type of risk assessment, we conducted a formal questionnaire-based risk assessment by e e o using tools from two NCCoE Cooperative Research and Development Agreement (CRADA) collaborators. c f h We conducted this questionnaire-based risk assessment to gain a greater understanding of the risks a r eg surrounding the wireless infusion pump ecosystem. The tool identifies the risks and maps them to the orf security controls. This type of risk assessment is considered appropriate for Tier 3: Information System, m h : per NIST’s RMF. One tool focuses on medical devices and the surrounding ecosystem, and the other tool t t p focuses on the HDO enterprise. Both questionnaire-based risk assessment tools leverage guidance and s /: / d best practices, including the NIST RMF and Cybersecurity Framework, and focus on built-in threats, o o.i vulnerabilities, and controls , , , . The assessment results measure the likelihood, r g 1/ severity, and impact of potential threats. 0 6. 0 2 All risk assessment activities provide an understanding of the challenges and risks involved when 8 / N integrating medical devices—in this case, wireless infusion pumps—into a typical IT network. Based on SI T S. this analysis, this project has two fundamental objectives: P 1. 8 0 ▪ protect the wireless infusion pumps from cyber attacks 0 -8 . ▪ protect the healthcare ecosystem, should a wireless infusion pump be compromised Per AAMI TIR57, “To assess security risk, several factors need to be identified and documented” . Based on our risk assessments and additional research, we identified primary threats, vulnerabilities, and risks that should be addressed when using wireless infusion pumps in HDOs. NIST SP 1800-8B: Securing Wireless Infusion Pumps 124.1.3 Assets Defining the asset is the first step in establishing the asset-threat-vulnerability construct necessary to properly evaluate or measure risks, per NIST’s RMF , , . An information asset is typically defined as a software application or information system that uses devices or third-party vendors for support and maintenance. For the NCCoE’s purposes, the information asset selected is a wireless infusion pump system. A risk assessment of this asset would include an evaluation of the cybersecurity controls for the pump, pump server, endpoint connections, network controls, data storage, remote access, vendor support, inventory control, and any other associated elements. T h p si 4.1.4 Threats u b acil Some potential known threats in HDOs that use network-connected medical devices, such as wireless t noi infusion pumps, are listed below. Refer to Appendix A for a description of each threat. si a av ▪ targeted attacks ali b el ▪ advanced persistent threats (APTs) rf e e ▪ disruption of service: denial-of-service (DoS) and distributed-denial-of-service (DDoS) attacks o c f h ▪ malware infections a r g rf e ▪ theft or loss of assets o m h : ▪ unintentional misuse t t sp ▪ vulnerable systems or devices directly connected to the device (e.g., via Universal-Serial-Bus /: / d [USB] or other hardwired, non-network connections) o o.i r It is important to understand that the threat landscape is constantly evolving, and that unknown threats g / 01 exist and may be unavoidable. These unknown threats need to be identified and remediated as soon as 6. 0 possible after they are found. 2 8 / N TSI 4.1.5 Vulnerabilities S. P 1. 8 Vulnerabilities afflict wireless infusion pump devices, pump management applications, network 0 0 -8 applications, and even the physical environment and personnel using the device or associated systems. . Within a complex system-of-systems environment, vulnerabilities may be exploited at all levels. The NVD is the United States (U.S.) government repository of standards-based vulnerability management data (https://nvd.nist.gov). NIST SP 1800-8B: Securing Wireless Infusion Pumps 144.1.6 Risks NIST SP 800-30, Guide for Conducting Risk Assessments, defines risk as, “a measure of the extent to which an entity is threatened by potential circumstance or event, and is typically a function of: (i) the adverse impacts that would arise if the circumstance or event occurs; and (ii) the likelihood of occurrence” . NIST SP 800-30 further notes, within a definition of risk assessment, that, “assessing risk requires careful analysis of threat and vulnerability information to determine the extent to which circumstances or events could adversely impact an organization and the likelihood that such circumstances or events will T h si occur.” p u b cil Based on the above guidance from NIST SP 800-30, several risks endanger medical devices: a t oi n ▪ Infusion pumps and server components may be leveraged for APTs and may serve as pivot si a points to cause adverse conditions throughout a hospital’s infrastructure. v a bali ▪ Infusion pumps may be manipulated to prevent the effective implementation of safety el rf measures, such as the drug library. e e o ▪ Infusion pump interfaces may be used for unintended or unexpected purposes, with those c f h conditions leading to degraded performance of the pump. a r g e rf ▪ PHI may be accessed remotely by unauthorized individuals. o m h : ▪ PHI may be disclosed to unauthorized individuals if the device is lost, stolen, or improperly t pt decommissioned. s /: / d ▪ Hospital’s network may have improper third-party vendor connections o o.i gr Although these risks may persist in infusion pumps and server components, HDOs should perform / 1 0 appropriate due diligence in determining the extent of the business impact and the likelihood of each 6. 20 risk factor. 8 / N SI Vulnerabilities may be present in infusion pumps and their server components, as these devices often T S. P include embedded operating systems on the endpoints. Infusion pumps are designed to maintain a 1. 08 prolonged period of useful life, and, as such, may include system components (e.g., an embedded 0 -8 operating system) that may reach either their end of life, or a period of degraded updates prior to the . infusion pump being retired from service. Patching and updating may become difficult over the course of time. Infusion pumps may not allow for the addition of third-party mechanisms, such as antivirus or anti- malware controls. Infusion pumps are validated medical devices, with set configuration and deployment specifications, and therefore may not accept third-party security controls or third-party-provided endpoint mitigation on the pump itself. Validation supports a manufacturer’s capabilities regarding the intended purpose of the device (i.e., infusing patients with medication, analgesics, or nutrients), and NIST SP 1800-8B: Securing Wireless Infusion Pumps 15requirements around the validation and approval process for medical devices fall under the auspices of the FDA. If limitations are identified in embedded operating systems used by an infusion pump, then vulnerabilities, weaknesses, and deficiencies may become known to malicious actors who may seek to leverage those deficiencies to install malicious or unauthorized software on those devices. Malicious software, or malware, may cause adverse conditions on the pump, degrading the performance of the pump or rendering the device unable to perform its function (e.g., ransomware; trojans that may allow for remote access to use the device as a pivot point; backdoors; malicious software that may allow for data exfiltration or may inappropriately consume system resources, T preventing the pump from rendering patient care functionality). Additionally, malware may be used to h si p convert the infusion pump into an access point for malicious actors to subsequently access or disrupt u b cil the operations of other hospital systems. a t oi n As noted above, infusion pumps may allow for the manipulation of configurations or safety measures si a implemented through the drug library (e.g., adjusting dosage or flow rates). This risk may be instantiated v a ali through local access, such as an interface or port on the device with either no or weak authentication or b el access control in place. Further, infusion pumps may be reachable across a hospital’s network, which rf ee provides an avenue for a malicious actor to cause an adverse event. o c f h Pumps may implement local ports, such as USB ports serial interfaces, Bluetooth, radio frequency, or a r g e other mechanisms that allow for close proximity connection to the pump. These ports may be rf o implemented with the intent to facilitate technical support; however, they also pose a risk by providing m h : a pathway for malicious actors to cause adverse conditions to the pump. t t p s /: Modern infusion pumps and server components may include PHI, such as a patient’s name, medical / d o.io record number (MRN), procedure coding, and medication or treatment. Through similar deficiencies r g that would allow configuration or use manipulation as noted above, this PHI may then be viewed, / 1 6.0 accessed, or removed by unauthorized individuals. Also, individuals who have direct access to the 0 2 infusion pump may be able to extract information through unsecured ports or interfaces , , , 8 / N SI , . T S. 1.P The following common vulnerabilities and control deficiencies may enable these risks: 8 0 0 -8 ▪ Implementation of default credentials and passwords: Weak authentication and default . passwords, or not implementing authentication or access control, may be discovered by malicious actors who would seek to cause adverse conditions. Malicious actors may leverage this control deficiency for risk factors that span from installing malware on the infusion pump, to manipulating configuration settings, to extracting information, such as PHI, from the device. ▪ Use of unsecured network ports, such as Telnet or File Transfer Protocol (FTP): Telnet and FTP are internet protocols that do not secure or encrypt network sessions. Telnet and FTP may be used nominally for technical support interfaces; however, malicious actors may attempt to leverage these protocols to access the infusion pump. Telnet and FTP may include deficiencies NIST SP 1800-8B: Securing Wireless Infusion Pumps 16that allow for the protocol itself to be compromised, and, because the network session is not encrypted, malicious actors may implement mechanisms to capture network sessions, including any authentication traffic, or to identify sensitive information, such as credentials, configuration information, or any PHI stored on the device. ▪ Local interfaces with limited security controls: Local interfaces, such as USB ports, serial ports, Bluetooth, radio frequency, or other ports may be used for device technical support. These ports, however, allow for malicious actors within close proximity to the device to access the device, to manipulate configuration settings, to access or remove data from the device, or to install malware on the device. These ports may exist on the pump for support purposes; T h however, use of the ports for unauthorized or unexpected purposes, such as recharging a si p mobile device (e.g., smart phone, tablet), may cause a disruption to the pump’s standard u b cil operation. a t oi n si 4.1.7 Recommendations and Best Practices a v a ali The recommendations provided in Appendix C address additional security concerns that, although not as b el pressing as those listed above, are worthy of consideration. If applied, these additional rf e recommendations will likely reduce risk factors or prevent them from becoming greater risks. Associated e o c f best practices for reducing the overall risk posture of infusion pumps are also included in Appendix C. h a r g e 4.2 Risk Response Strategy rf o m h : Risk mitigation is often confused with risk response. Per NIST SP 800-30, risk mitigation is defined as t t p “prioritizing, evaluating, and implementing the appropriate risk-reducing controls/countermeasures s /: / recommended from the risk management process.” d o o.i gr Risk mitigation is a subset of risk response. Risk response is defined by NIST SP 800-30 as accepting; / 1 0 avoiding; mitigating; sharing, or transferring risks. When considering risk response, your organization 6. 20 should recommend, to a corporate risk management board, ways that the Information Risk Manager (or 8 / N equivalent) should treat risk. SI T S. P 1. 4.2.1 Risk Mitigation 8 0 0 -8 Organizations must determine their tolerance or appetite for risk—the response to which will drive risk . remediation or risk mitigation for identified risks. This tolerance should be codified in a Risk Management Plan, which will include regulatory requirements and guidance, industry best practices, and security controls. Organizations should set an appropriate risk tolerance based on the factors noted above, with the intent to remediate those risks above the established risk tolerance (i.e., critical or high risks.) These remediation responses can take the form of administrative, physical, and technical controls, or an appropriate mix. As previously mentioned, Appendix C identifies several mitigation recommendations NIST SP 1800-8B: Securing Wireless Infusion Pumps 17regarding specific risk. Additional compensating safeguards, countermeasures, or controls are noted below: ▪ physical security controls, including standard tamper-evident physical seals, which can be applied to hardware to indicate unauthorized physical access ,  ▪ ensuring the implementation of a physical asset management program that manages and tracks unique, mobile media, such as removable flash memory devices (e.g., Secure Digital [SD] cards, thumb drives) used by pump software hosted on an endpoint client. Consider the encryption of all portable media used in such a fashion , , , . T sih ▪ following procedures for clearing wireless network authentication credentials on the endpoint p u client if the pump is to be removed or transported from the facility. These procedures can be b cil found in pump user manuals, but should be referenced in official HDO policies and procedures a t oi , , , . n si a ▪ changing wireless network authentication credentials regularly and, if there is evidence of v a ali unauthorized access to a pump system, immediately changing network authentication b el credentials ,  rf e e ▪ ensuring that all wireless network access is minimally configured for Wi-Fi Protected Access II o c f (WPA2) Pre-Shared Key (PSK) encryption and authentication. All pumps should be set to WPA2 h ra encryption , , , . g e orf ▪ ensuring that all patching has been applied, including those components that will use WPA2 m h : ▪ All pumps and pump systems should include cryptographic modules that have been validated as t t p s meeting NIST Federal Information Processing Standards (FIPS) Publication 140-2 . /: / d o ▪ All ports are disabled, except when in use, and the device has no listening ports , , , o.i gr , . / 1 0 6. ▪ employing mutual transport layer security (TLS) encryption in transit between the client and 0 82 server  / N SI ▪ employing individual pump authentication with no shared key for all pumps ,  T S. P 1. ▪ certificate-based authentication for a pump server , , ,  8 0 0 -8 During the course of this project, several vulnerabilities were published in the NVD (https://nvd.nist.gov) . that identified means by which malicious actors may remotely compromise WPA2-secured sessions through the use of “key reinstallation attacks” (KRACKs). Individuals should review noted WPA2 vulnerabilities, refer to vendor/manufacturer patching and updates, and apply those patches and updates as soon as possible. 4.3 Security Characteristics and Controls Mapping As described in the previous sections, we derived the security characteristics by analyzing risk in collaboration with our healthcare-sector stakeholders as well as our participating vendor partners. In our example implementation, we did not observe any security characteristics that mapped to the Respond or Recover Subcategories of the NIST Cybersecurity Framework. P 1. 8 0 0 .-8 NIST SP 1800-8B: Securing Wireless Infusion Pumps 355 Architecture Wireless infusion pumps are no longer standalone devices; they now include pump servers for managing the pumps, drug libraries, networks allowing for interoperability with other hospital systems, and VPN tunnels to outside organizations for maintenance. While interconnectivity, enhanced communications, and safety measures on the pump have added complexity to infusion pumps, these components can help improve patient outcomes and safety. As infusion pumps have evolved, one safety mechanism development was the invention of the “drug T h library.” The drug library is a mechanism that is applied to an infusion pump and that catalogs si p medications, fluids, dosage, and flow rates. While hospital pharmacists may be involved in the u b cil maintenance of the drug library, continuous application of the drug library to the infusion pump a t oi environment tends to be managed through a team of biomedical engineers. Initially, the drug library file n si may be loaded onto the pump through a communication port. When the drug library file is updated, all a v a infusion pumps need to be updated to ensure that they adhere to the current rendition of that drug ali elb library. Drug library distribution, which may require that staff manually adjust individual pumps, may erf become onerous for the biomedical staff in HDOs that use thousands of pumps , . e o c f Manufacturers provide wireless communications on some pumps, and use a pump server to manage the h a gr drug library file, capture usage information on the pumps, and provide pump updates. e rf o m Medical device manufacturers are subject to regulatory practices by the FDA, and may tend to focus on th : the primary function of the pump (i.e., assurance that the pump delivers fluids of a certain volume and t p /:s defined flow rates, consistent with needs that providers may have to ensure safe and appropriate / d o patient care). Technology considerations, such as cybersecurity controls, may not be primarily addressed o.i r in the device design and approval process. As such, infusion pumps may include technology that does g / 01 not lend itself to the same controls that an HDO may implement on standard desktops, laptops, or 6. 0 workstations used for productivity , . 2 8 / N SI As technology has evolved, cybersecurity risk has expanded, both in visibility and in the number of T PS. threats and vulnerabilities. This expansion has led to a heightened concern, from manufacturers and the 1. 8 FDA, and work has been established to identify measures to better respond to cybersecurity risk , , 0 0 -8 . In Section 5.1, we describe the wireless infusion pump ecosystem by defining the components. . Section 5.2 discusses the data flow, and Section 5.3 explains the set of controls that we use in our example implementation, including those for networks, pumps, pump servers, and enterprise. Section 5.4 describes the target architecture for our example implementation. NIST SP 1800-8B: Securing Wireless Infusion Pumps 365.1 Basic System A basic wireless infusion pump ecosystem includes a wireless infusion pump, a pump server, a network consisting of an AP, a firewall, and a VPN to a manufacturer (Figure 5-1). Figure 5-1 Basic System T h si p u b cil a t oi n si a v a ali b el rf e e o c f h a r g e rf o m h : t t p s /: / d o o.i r g / 1 0 06. 2 8 / N SI 5.2 Data Flow T S. P 1. The flow of data between a wireless infusion pump and its corresponding server falls into the following 8 0 0 transaction categories: -8 . ▪ modifying the drug library ▪ performing software updates ▪ remotely managing the devices ▪ auditing the data flow processes Infusion pumps may also include other advanced features, such as auto-programming to receive patient prescription information and to record patient treatment information to the patient’s EHR. NIST SP 1800-8B: Securing Wireless Infusion Pumps 375.3 Cybersecurity Controls This section discusses security controls by their location, either on the network, pump, or pump server. We also describe controls implemented in the NCCoE lab, and depict the controls implemented in our final architecture. In general, we recommend that a clinically focused network be designed to protect the information used in HDOs, whether that information is at-rest or in-transit. As described in Cisco Medical-Grade Network (MGN) 2.0-Security Architectures, no single architecture can be designed to meet the security requirements of all organizations . However, many cybersecurity best practices can be applied by T h si HDOs to meet regulatory compliance standards. p u b cil Our reference architecture uses Cisco’s solution architecture as the baseline. This baseline demonstrates a t oi how the network can be used to provide multi-tiered protection for medical devices when exchanging n si information via a network connection. The goal of our reference architecture is to provide a v a countermeasures to deal with challenges identified in the assessment process. For our use-case ali elb solution, we use segmentation and defense-in-depth as security models to build and maintain a secure erf device infrastructure. This section provides additional details on how to employ security strategies to e o achieve specific targeted protections when securing wireless infusion pumps. c f h a gr We used the following cybersecurity controls: e rf o m ▪ network controls h : tt ▪ pump controls p s /: / d ▪ pump server controls o o.i r ▪ enterprise-level controls g / 1 0 06. 5.3.1 Network Controls 2 8 / N SI Proper network segmentation or network zoning is essential to developing a strong cybersecurity T S. posture , , , , . Segmentation uses network devices, such as switches and firewalls, to P 81. split a large computer network into subnetworks, each referred to as a network segment . Network 0 0 -8 segmentation not only enhances network management, but also improves cybersecurity, allowing for . the separation of networks based on network security requirements driven by business needs or asset value. NIST SP 1800-8B: Securing Wireless Infusion Pumps 38The architecture designed for this build uses Cisco’s solution architecture as the baseline for demonstrating how the network can be used to provide multi-tiered protection for medical devices when exchanging information with the outside world during the operation involving network communication. The goal of this architecture design is to provide countermeasures to mitigate challenge areas identified in the assessment process. In our use-case solution, segmentation and defense-in-depth are the security models that we used as security measures to build and maintain secure device infrastructure. This section provides additional details on how to employ security strategies to achieve the target security characteristics for securing wireless infusion pumps. hT 5.3.1.1 Segmentation/Zoning si p u b Our network architecture uses a zone-based security approach. By using different local networks for cil a designated purposes, networked equipment identified for a specific purpose can be put together on the t oi n same network segment and protected with an internal firewall. The implication is that there is no si a inherent trust between network zones and that trust limitations are enforced by properly configuring v a ali firewalls to protect equipment in one zone from other, less-trusted zones. By limiting access from other, b rf el less-trusted areas, firewalls can more effectively protect the enterprise network. e e o For discussion purposes, we include some generic components of a typical HDO in our network c f ah architecture examples. A given healthcare facility may be simpler or more complex and may contain r g e different subcomponents. The generic architecture contains several functional segments, including the rf mo following elements: h : t pt ▪ core network s /: / d ▪ guest network o o.i gr ▪ business office / 1 0 6. ▪ database server 0 2 /8 ▪ enterprise services N SI S.T ▪ clinical services P 1. 8 ▪ biomedical engineering 0 0 .-8 ▪ medical devices with wireless LAN ▪ remote access for external vendor support At a high level, each zone is implemented as a VLAN with a combination firewall/router Cisco ASA device connecting it to the rest of the enterprise through a backbone network, referred to as the core network , , . Segments may consist of physical or virtual networks. For simplicity and convenience, we implemented subnets that correspond exactly to VLANs. The routing configuration is the same for each subnet, but the firewall configuration may vary depending on each zone’s specific purpose. An external router/firewall device is used to connect the enterprise and guest network to the internet. NIST SP 1800-8B: Securing Wireless Infusion Pumps 39Segmentation is implemented via a VLAN by using Cisco switches. The following subsections provide a short description of each segment and the final network architecture. 5.3.1.1.1 Core Network Zone Our reference architecture implements a core network zone that consists of the equipment and systems used to establish the backbone network infrastructure. The external firewall/router also has an interface connected to the core enterprise network, just like other firewall/router devices in the other zones. This zone serves as the backbone of the enterprise network and consists only of routers connected by switches. The routers automatically share internal route information with each other via authenticated hT Open Shortest Path First (OSPF) to mitigate configuration errors as zones are added or removed. si p u b 5.3.1.1.2 Guest Network Zone cil a Hospitals often implement a guest network that allows visitors or patients to access internet services t oi n during their visit. As shown in Figure 5-2 (Section 5.3.1.1.10), network traffic here tends not to be clinical si a in nature, but is offered as a courtesy to hospital visitors and patients to access the internet. Refer to v a ali Section 5.3.1.5, External Access, for additional technical details. b el erf 5.3.1.1.3 Business Office Zone e c fo A business office zone is established for systems dedicated to hospital office productivity and does not h a include direct patient-facing systems. This zone consists of traditional clients on an enterprise network, r g rf e such as workstations, laptops, and possibly mobile devices. Within the enterprise, the business office o m zone will primarily interact with the enterprise services zone. This zone may also include Wi-Fi access. h : t t p 5.3.1.1.4 Database Server Zone s /: / A database server zone is established to house server components that support data persistence. The d o o.i database server zone may include data stores that aggregate potentially sensitive information, and, r g / given the volume, require safeguards. Because databases may include PHI, HIPAA privacy and security 1 0 6. controls are applicable. This zone consists of servers with databases. Ideally, applications in the 0 2 8 enterprise services zone and biomedical engineering zone use these databases, instead of storing / N SI information on application servers. This type of centralization allows for a simplified management of T PS. security controls to protect the information stored in databases. 1. 8 0 0 -8 5.3.1.1.5 Enterprise Services Zone . The enterprise services zone consists of systems that support hospital staff productivity. Enterprise services may not be directly patient-specific systems, but rather support core office functions found in a hospital. This zone consists of traditional enterprise services, such as the domain name system (DNS), Active Directory, Identity Service System, and asset inventory that probably lives in a server room or data center. These services must be accessible from various other zones in the enterprise. NIST SP 1800-8B: Securing Wireless Infusion Pumps 405.3.1.1.6 Clinical Services Zone The clinical services zone consists of systems that pertain to providing patient care. Examples of systems that would be hosted in this zone include the EHR system, pharmacy systems, health information systems, and other clinical systems to support patient care. 5.3.1.1.7 Biomedical Engineering Zone The biomedical engineering zone establishes a separate area that enables a biomedical engineering team to manage and maintain systems, such as medical devices, as shown in Figure 5-2 (Section 5.3.1.1.10). This zone consists of all equipment needed to provision and maintain medical T devices. In the case of wireless infusion pumps, this is where the pump management servers are hosted h si p on the network. u b cil a 5.3.1.1.8 Medical Device Zone t oi n The medical device zone provides a network space where medical devices may be hosted. Infusion si a pumps would be deployed in this zone. Infusion pump systems are designed so that all external v a ali connections to EHR systems or vendor maintenance operations can be completed through an associated b el pump server that resides in the biomedical engineering zone. Access to the rest of the network and rf e e internet is blocked. This zone contains a dedicated wireless network to support the wireless infusion o c f pumps, as explained in Section 5.3.1.2, Medical Device Zone’s Wireless LAN. h a r g rf e 5.3.1.1.9 Remote Access Zone mo The remote access zone provides a network segment that extends external privileged access so that h : vendors may access their manufactured components and systems on the broader HDO network. The Wi-Fi management network is different, in that it does not have a firewall/router that connects 0 82 directly to the core network, as shown in Figure 5-3. This is a completely closed network used for the / N SI management and communication between the Cisco Aironet wireless AP and the Cisco WLC. The WLC is T S. the central point where wireless Service Set Identifiers (SSIDs), VLANs, and WPA2 security settings are P 1. 8 managed for the entire enterprise , , , , , , , , , . 0 0 -8 . Two SSIDs were defined: IP_Dev and IP_Dev Cert. IP_Dev uses WPA2-PSK, and IP_Dev Cert uses WPA2- Enterprise protocols. In an actual HDO, two WLCs should be configured for redundancy. Initially, the wireless APs configure themselves for network connectivity, like any other device using Dynamic Host Configuration Protocol (DHCP) from the switch DHCP server (see the green line in Figure 5-3). The switch also sends DHCP Option 43, which provides the Internet Protocol (IP) address of the WLC. The AP then connects to the WLC to automatically download firmware updates and wireless configuration information. Finally, the CAPWAP tunnel is formed to encrypt wireless traffic (see the black line in Figure 5-3). The traffic is then routed to the enterprise network via the WLC , , , . NIST SP 1800-8B: Securing Wireless Infusion Pumps 42Figure 5-3 Wi-Fi Management T h si p u b cil a t oi n si a v a ali b el rf e e o c f h a r g e When a device first connects to the Wi-Fi network, it needs to authenticate with either the agreed-upon rf o PSK or certificate. The authentication process is tunneled from the AP back to the WLC, as shown in m h : Figure 5-4. In the case of a PSK, the WLC verifies that the client key matches (see the green line in t t p s Figure 5-4). In the case of a certificate, the authentication process is passed from the WLC to the Cisco /: / d ISE for validation by using remote authentication dial-in user service (RADIUS) protocol (see the yellow o o.i line in Figure 5-4). NIST SP 1800-8B: Securing Wireless Infusion Pumps 44Figure 5-5 Wi-Fi Device Access T h si p u b cil a t oi n si a v a ali b el rf e e o c f h a gr Using an enterprise-grade Wi-Fi system can simplify transitions to more secure protocols by decoupling e orf Wi-Fi SSIDs and security parameters from the Wi-Fi spectrum and physical Ethernet connections. First, m h : every AP only needs to broadcast on a single Wi-Fi channel (in each band) and can broadcast multiple t pt SSIDs. This helps avoid interference due to multiple independent wireless systems trying to use the s //: same frequencies. Second, each SSID can be tied to its own VLAN. This means that logical network d o o.i separation can be maintained in Wi-Fi without having to use additional spectrum. Third, multiple SSIDs r g / can be tied to the same VLAN or standard Ethernet network. Each SSID can have its own security 1 0 6. configuration as well. For example, in our use case, we have two different authentication mechanisms 0 2 8 for granting access to the same network: one configured for WPA2-PSK, and the other for so-called / N SI enterprise certificates. This can be particularly useful for gradual transitions from old security T PS. mechanisms (e.g., Wireless Encryption Protocol [WEP], Wi-Fi Protected Access [WPA]) or old PSKs to 1. 8 newer ones, instead of needing to transition all devices at one time. In our case, to determine which 0 0 -8 devices may need reconfiguration to use certificates, we used the WLC to identify exactly which devices . are using old PSK SSIDs. Once this number is reduced to an acceptable level, the old PSK SSID can be turned off, and only certificate-based authentication will be allowed. NIST SP 1800-8B: Securing Wireless Infusion Pumps 455.3.1.3 Network Access Control This section describes how network access control using a wireless LAN, as shown above, is applied to the wireless infusion pumps. Before we describe network access controls, it is important to discuss each pump’s wireless protection protocol. There are three available wireless protection protocols (WEP, WPA, and WPA2). We also describe in-depth options for WPA2-PSK. Finally, we describe options for WPA2 across the HDO enterprise. Many of the infusion pumps used in this NCCoE project are newer models, capable of supporting various wireless protocols. For HDOs, WPA2 is the recommended wireless protocol to use. T h si WEP and WPA are considered insufficient for appropriately securing wireless network sessions. Our p u architecture is designed to support multiple levels of access control for different groups of users. The b acil architecture is configured to use WPA2-PSK and WPA2-Enterprise security protocols for secure wireless t oi n connections to accommodate the best available security mechanisms, depending on which vendor si a products your organization uses. Please note that a wireless infusion pump manufactured prior to 2004 v a ali may not be able to support these newer wireless security protocols . b el rf The WPA2-PSK is often referred to as PSK mode. This protocol is designed for small office networks and e e o does not require an external authentication server. Each wireless network device encrypts the network c f h traffic by using a 256-bit key. All pumps used in our example implementation support this wireless a r eg security mode, and each pump performed properly when using this mode. However, because all devices rf o share the same key in a PSK mode using WPA2-PSK, if credentials are compromised, then significant m h : manual reconfiguration and change management will be required. t t p s /: WPA2 enterprise security uses 802.1x/EAP. By using 802.1x, an HDO can leverage the existing network / d o.io infrastructure’s centralized authentication services, such as a RADIUS authentication server, to provide a gr strong client authentication. Cisco recommends that WPA2 Enterprise, which uses the Advanced / 1 6.0 Encryption Standard (AES) cypher for optimum encryption, be used for wireless medical devices, if 0 2 available. We implemented WPA2 Enterprise with EAP-TLS security mode on several of our pumps to 8 / N demonstrate that these pumps can leverage the public key infrastructure (PKI) to offer strong endpoint SI T S. authentication and the strongest encryption possible for highly secure wireless transmissions. In this P 1. mode, pumps were authenticated to the wireless network with a client certificate issued by DigiCert 8 0 0 -8 Certificate Authority. During the authentication process, the pump’s certificates are validated against a . RADIUS authentication server using Cisco ISE. Automatic logoff features allow the system to terminate the endpoints from the network after a predetermined time of inactivity. Organizations manage and control the client certificates via the certificate authority. With this capability, organizations may revoke and renew certificates as needed. Once WPA2 is selected as the appropriate wireless protection protocol, certificates may be issued to authenticate infusion pumps by using 802.1x/EAP-TLS mode, as illustrated in Figure 5-6 , , , , , , , , , , , , , , , . Established connections are persistent to facilitate IT operations, enforce security, and maintain 0 2 8 comprehensive audit trails. P Through a standard web browser, ConsoleWorks can be securely accessed from any geographical 1. 08 location, eliminating the need for administrators and engineers to be locally present to perform their 0 -8 work. . NIST SP 1800-8B: Securing Wireless Infusion Pumps 515.3.2 Pump Controls Wireless infusion pumps have the following controls: ▪ endpoint protection ▪ hardening ▪ data protection 5.3.2.1 Endpoint Protection T sih Traditional security relies on the network border to provide security protection to its internal nodes, p u using security technologies, such as application firewalls, proxy gateways, centralized virus scan, and b cil network IDS and IPS. The challenge faced here, however, is that medical devices, such as wireless a t noi infusion pumps, may not have the capability to have third-party tools installed or deployed to effectively a si provide control. As such, endpoint protection is applied to that equipment where possible. This may v a ali limit endpoint protection controls to servers or workstations that may operate in the hospital b el infrastructure. Nodes, such as networked medical devices, should participate in their own security. rf e Otherwise, the device may become the weakest element in the enterprise and present a risk to the e o c f entire HDO network. h a r g To avoid the single point of failure caused by an unsecured node, every system should have an e orf appropriate combination of local protections applied to it. These protections include code signing, anti- m h : tampering, encryption, access control, whitelisting, and others. Controls are layered across a technology t t p stack, with the intent to improve the overall cybersecurity posture, recognizing that there may be s /: / d limitations to applying a full set of controls for each node. o o.i r g 5.3.2.2 Hardening / 1 0 6. 0 Wireless infusion pumps and their servers are considered computing endpoints, when it comes to 2 8 / hardening the software contained within these devices. Medical devices may contain third-party N TSI products, including proprietary or commercial embedded operating systems, network communication S. 1.P modules, runtime environments, web services, or databases. Because these products can contain 8 0 vulnerabilities, medical devices may also inherit these vulnerabilities just by using the products , , 0 .-8 , , . Therefore, it is important to identify all software applications used on medical devices, implement securing and hardening procedures recommended by the manufacturers, and apply timely patches and updates to guard against any newly discovered threats. Hardening may include the following actions: ▪ disabling unused or unnecessary communication ports and services ▪ changing manufacturer default administrative passwords ▪ securing remote APs, if there are any NIST SP 1800-8B: Securing Wireless Infusion Pumps 52▪ confirming that the firmware version is up-to-date ▪ ensuring that hashes or digital signatures are valid However, please note that most infusion pumps do not have the same level of storage resources and Central Processing Unit (CPU) processing capability as those provided for personal computers and servers. Hardening or modifying devices, configurations, or settings should be performed based on guidance from the manufacturer. Wireless infusion pumps are medical devices that adhere to FDA regulation, where the manufacturer has validated appropriate functionality based on a defined configuration. T h si Identified vulnerabilities should be disclosed to the manufacturer, who may advise on appropriate p u b mitigation approaches and provide patches, fixes, and updates where appropriate. cil a t noi 5.3.2.3 Data Protection si a v The two primary reasons for data protection are confidentiality and integrity. Medical devices may a bali contain patient data, such as the patient’s name, MRN, gender, age, height, weight, procedure number, el rf medication and treatment information, or other identifiers that may constitute PHI. PHI must be e o e appropriately protected (e.g., through encryption or other safeguard measures that would prevent hc f unauthorized disclosure of such information). a r g e rf Infusion pumps may also contain configuration data, such as drug libraries specifying dosage and o m threshold limits. This data must be protected against compromises as well. Our defense-in-depth h : t approach for data integrity involves sandboxing the critical system files stored in pump servers by using t p /:s DCS:SA and by encrypting messages when communicating between a medical infusion pump and the / d o backend infusion management system, via Internet Protocol Security or secure-sockets-layer encryption o.i gr (e.g., HTTPS, TLS). / 1 0 6. 0 5.3.3 Pump Server Controls 2 8 / N SI Pump server features vary. Usually, a pump server can be used to distribute firmware, the drug library, T PS. or other software updates used inside the devices, or as a tool for providing services, such as reporting 1. 8 and device asset management. Data collected by the infusion pump server is valuable for further 0 0 -8 analysis to provide reports on trends, compliance checking, and to measure infusion safety. . Because pump servers connect to infusion pumps to deliver and receive infusion-related information, it is also important to secure the infusion pump server and its associated applications, databases, and communication channels. NIST SP 1800-8B: Securing Wireless Infusion Pumps 535.3.3.1 User Account Controls Access to the pump server typically implements username/password authentication. After the pump server is installed, an initial step is to define the password policy that applies to users accessing the pump server. When managing user accounts for a pump server, common cybersecurity hygiene should include the following actions: ▪ changing factory default passwords ▪ enforcing password policies T h ▪ assigning each user’s access level by using the least-privilege principle si p u ▪ if supported, using centralized access management, such as LDAP, for user account b acil management at the enterprise level APT: t oi si n ▪ configuring automatic logoff a v a ali 5.3.3.2 Communication Controls b el rf Pump servers interface with many other systems or components, such as databases, web services, and e e o web portals. Communications between different systems can be configured. Pump servers might hc f provide choices for selecting unsecure or secure Transport Control Protocol (TCP)/IP ports for a r g communication. We recommend using secure (e.g., stateful, encrypted network sessions) ports for e orf message communication or for package download. m h : t There may be a default setting for the communication interval, in number of seconds, for t p s /: communication attempts between the server and the pump. Be sure to properly set this idle timeout / d o setting. o.i r g / 1 5.3.3.3 Application Protection 0 6. 0 2 Application protection refers to software applications running on the pump servers. Most of the 8 / N SI software application security concerns and security controls that are used on traditional personal T S. computers and servers may also be applied to pump servers to protect data integrity and confidentiality. P 81. These control measures may include those listed below: 0 0 -8 . ▪ trusted applications ▪ stronger access control mechanisms for pumps and pump servers ▪ better key management ▪ application whitelisting ▪ sandboxing applications ▪ performing code-signing verification for newly installed software NIST SP 1800-8B: Securing Wireless Infusion Pumps 54▪ applying the latest patches and software updates ▪ encrypting message data in transit or data at rest Server security baseline integrity is achieved via the use of three Symantec cybersecurity products on an enterprise network with a specific focus on wireless infusion pumps: ▪ DCS:SA ▪ SEP ▪ APT:N T h si Each of these Symantec products provides protections for components in the enterprise systems in p u b different levels. With pre-built policies, the DCS:SA server that is installed can provide out-of-the-box cil a HIDS and HIPS by monitoring and preventing suspicious server activities on pump servers. The use of t oi n DCS:SA also provides the host firewall service for controlling inbound and outbound network traffic to si va and from a protected server. Using DCS:SA, the configuration settings, files, and file systems in the pump a ali server can be locked down using policy-based least-privilege access controls to restrict application and b rf el operating-system behavior and to prevent file and system tampering. e e c fo Like DCS:SA, SEP provides similar protection for endpoint devices and servers. SEP features in-memory ah exploit mitigation and antivirus file protection to block malware from infecting protected endpoint r g e servers. This will reduce the possibility of zero-day exploits on popular software that may not have been rf o m properly patched or updated. To protect endpoint servers, an SEP agent must be installed on servers. h : t pt ATP:N can provide network-based protection of medical device subnets by monitoring internal inbound s //: and outbound internet traffic. It can also be used as a dashboard to gain visibility to all devices and all d o o.i network protocols. In addition, if ATP:N is integrated with the SEP, ATP:N can then monitor and manage r /g all network traffic from the endpoints and provide threat assessments for dangerous activity to secure 1 0 6. medical devices on an enterprise network. The use of these Symantec security products is depicted in 0 2 8 Figure 5-10. As depicted, the ATP:N will inspect all traffic going through the core network switch via port / N SI mirroring between the enterprise services, biomedical engineering, and medical device zones. Wired T S. traffic within each zone is also inspected via port mirroring on the switches used in those zones. P 1. 8 0 0 -8 . NIST SP 1800-8B: Securing Wireless Infusion Pumps 55Figure 5-10 Pump Server Protection T h si p u b cil a t oi n si a v a ali b el rf e e o c f h a r g e rf o m h : t t p s /: / d o o.i r g / 1 0 6. 0 2 8 / 5.3.4 Enterprise-Level Controls N SI T S. P 5.3.4.1 Asset Tracking and Inventory Control 1. 8 0 0 Medical asset management includes asset tracking and asset inventory control. Asset tracking is a -8 . management process used to maintain oversight of the equipment, using anything from simple methods (such as pen and paper), to record equipment, to more-sophisticated information technology asset management (ITAM) platforms. HDOs can use asset tracking to verify that a device is still in the possession of the assigned, authorized users. Some more-advanced tracking solutions may provide service for locating missing or stolen devices. Inventory management is also important throughout a medical device’s life cycle. Inventory tracking should not be limited to hardware inventory management. It should also be expanded to include software, software versions, and data stored and accessed in the devices, for security purposes. HDOs NIST SP 1800-8B: Securing Wireless Infusion Pumps 56can use this type of inventory information to verify compliance with security guidelines and to check for exposure of confidential information to unauthorized entities. 5.3.4.2 Monitoring and Audit Controls Logging, monitoring, and auditing procedures are essential security measures that can be used to help HDOs prevent incidents and provide an effective response when a security breach occurs. The activities can include: ▪ logging - recording events to appropriate logs T h si ▪ monitoring - overseeing the events for abnormal activities, such as scanning, compromises, p u malicious code, and DoSs in real time b cil ta ▪ auditing - reviewing and checking these recorded events to find abnormal situations or to oi si n evaluate if the applied security measures are effective. a av By combining the logging, monitoring, and auditing features, an organization will be able to track, ali b record, review, and respond to abnormal activities, and provide historical records when needed. el erf Many malware and virus infections can be almost completely avoided by using properly configured e o c f firewalls or proxies with regularly updated knowledge databases and filters to prevent connections to h a known malicious domains. It is also important to review your firewall logs for blocked connection r g rf e attempts so that you can identify the attached source and remedy infected devices if needed. o m h : In our example implementation, user audit controls—simple audits—are in place. Although additional t pt SIEM tools and centralized log aggregation tools are recommended to maximize security event analysis s /: / capabilities, aggregation and analytics tools like these are considered out of scope for this project d o o.i iteration. r g / 1 0 Each system is monitored for compliance with a secure configuration baseline. Each system is also 6. 20 monitored for risks to known good, secure configurations by vulnerability scanning tools. In our project, 8 / N the Cisco AP, the Cisco ISE as the RADIUS authentication server, the TDi VendorNet, and the pump SI S.T servers from each vendor, are all equipped with proper monitoring and logging capabilities. Real-time P 1. monitoring for events happening within these systems can be analyzed and compared to the baseline. If 8 0 0 any abnormal behavior occurs, it can be detected. The auditing of data was considered out of scope for -8 . this reference design because the absence of an actual data center made auditing behavior impractical. 5.4 Final Architecture The target architecture, depicted in Figure 5-11, indicates the implementation of network segmentation and controls as described by this practice guide. Segmentation identified nine zones, ranging from the guest network zone to the medical device zone, and includes zones for the Wi-Fi infrastructure and the core network infrastructure. The zoned concept implements firewall/router devices to enforce segmentation, with the firewall enforcing limited trust relationships between each zone. For example, NIST SP 1800-8B: Securing Wireless Infusion Pumps 57access between the biomedical engineering zone and the medical device zone is limited to only the ports identified by the vendor and the associated pump server. Noted in the diagram (Figure 5-11) are processes that have impact on the overall architecture. Security controls are implemented to enforce encryption on network sessions. For Wi-Fi, leveraging standard protocols, such as WPA2-PSK and WPA2 Enterprise, created a secure channel for the pumps to communicate with the APs, and to use TLS to secure the communication channel from the pumps to the server.
